Sponsoring Agency Cod.\n\n15, Sypplementary Notes\n\nThis paper documents the basic ideas and constructs for a general physical/\nphysiological process level winter wheat simulation model. \t It is a materials balance model\nwhich calculates daily increments of photosynthate production and respiratory losses in the crop canopy.\t It simulates the partitioning of the resulting dry matter to the active growing\ntissues in the plant each day.\t It simulates transpiration and the uptake of nitrogen from\nthe soil profile.\t It incorporates the RHIZOS model which simulates, in two dimensions, the\nmovement of water,\t roots and soluble nutrients through the soil profile.\t It records the\ntime of initiation of each of the plant organs. \t These pheiological events are calculated\nfrom temperature functions with delays resulting from physiological, stress. \t Stress is\ndefined mathematically as an imbalance in the metabolite supplycdemand ratio. \t Physiological\nstress is also the basis for the calculation of rates of tiller and floret abortion.\t Thus,\ntillering and head differentiation are modeled as the resultants of the two processes,\nmorphogenesis and abortion which \t may be occurring simultaneously.\n\nte. documents the basic ideas and constructs \'for a general\nphysical /physiological\n\nprocess level winter wheat simulation model.\n\nit is a materials,! balance model which calculates daily increments of\n\nphotosynthate production-and respiratory losses in the crop cano\'y.\nIt simulates the partitioning of the resulting dry matter to the\nactive growing tissues in the plant each clay. \t it simulates\ntranspiration and the uptake of nitrogen from the soil profile_\t It\nincorporates the RHIZOS model which simulates, in two dimensions, the\nmovement of water, rooms and soluble nutrients through the soil\n\n4\n\nprofile..\t\n\nIt records the time of\n\ninitiation of each of the plant\n\norgans.\t These phenological events are calculated from teipperature\nfunctions with delays resulting from physiological stress. \t Stress is\ndefined mathematically as an imbalance in the metabolite supply:demand\nratio.\t Physiological stress is also the basis for the calculation of\nrates of tiller and floret abortion.\t Thus, tillering and head\ndifferentiation are modeled as the resultants of the two processes,\nwrphogenesis and abortion which may be occurring simultaneously.\ns\n\nfi\n\ny\n\nr\n\nq\n\nr\n\n}\n\n}\n\n-\n\nY\n\nl^\n\ni\n\nr\n\nCONMUS\npage\nIntroduction and objectives\n\n1\n\nGeneral Model Strategy, Characteristics, Features and et.al., 1978).\t As has been noted elsewhere (Baker,\net.al., 1982, Pye et.al ., 1982, Marani and Baker, 1981) #, the\nfeasibility of \'building simulation models of plant growth and yield\nhas recently been demonstrated and \'models of cotton, corn, alfalfar\nsoybean, peanut, sugar beet, winter wheat, .rice and sorghum are now.\navailable. Such models have been developed at research locations in\nthe US, England, Australia, the Netherlands, USSR, and .japan.\t Most of\nthis work may be viewed as a natural extension of the growth analysis\nwork in England beginning with Fisher (1921) and Gregory (1917) and\t\nthe later work o,f Watson\'(1947), and in the USSR ., the work of\nNichiporovich (1954).\t The experimental research in crop canopy\n\n}\n\nphotosynthesis of Musgrave and his students in the US (Noss, et.al.,\n1961, Baker and Musgrave, 1964),; and that of Mwata (1961) and others\nin Japan, Duncan, et.al ,, (1967) in the US, deWit (1965) in the\nNetherlands, and Ross (1969), and Tooming (1967) in the USSR\nimmediately precede our work in the effort to predict growth and yield\nof field crops.\nour objective in developing WINTER WMT is to identify\nassemble the factors determining winter wheat growth and yield in a\nformat which will aid system design (breeding and new cultural\npractices, and combinations thereof), crop management decision making\t\nat the farm level, and yield forecasting.\t Thus, we see this effort as\nan ongoing process of identifying and mathematically testing\n(sensitivity analysis) the factors determining winter wheat growth and\nyield, and, of synthesis in which these factors are assembled for\nrational use by agronomists and farm managers.\nGeneral Model Strategy, Characteristics\nFeatures and Rationale\n\n\'r\n\nSince winter, wheat has tremendous ecological, ranga, the above\nobjective implies a general model capable of simulating crop growth.\nover the widest poesi.15le range of climates and soils.\t Since different\n\n`\n\'\n\n{\n\'\n\n(\n\nIr\n\nenvironmental factors affect different physiological and physical\nprocesses in different ways and because we view the model development\nas an ongoing affair in which new ideas and information about the crop\nare incorporated as needed, and as they become available, a process related modular structure was indicated.\nThe model is dynamic because photosynthesis, respiration, growth,\nand water flow change rapidly with temperature, light intensity, and\nplant water status.\t Except for pollen dessication and organ abscissioh, the plant processes are continuous, so, the model must be essentially continuous.\t However, we have found it permissible and appropriate to.use discrete time steps which, depending an the process\nbeing simulated, vary in length.\t This permits great savings in the\ncomputer cost of running the model.\t Length of the time steps (for various processes) mist be determined mathematically, evaluating size and\ndistribution of errors generated by using progressively longer time\n\na\n\nr\n\nsteps.\n\nWINTER WHET, like most crop simulators of plant growth, is a\nmaterials balance model,\t The plant model contains pools of nitrogen\nair:d labile carbohydrates which arrive via the transpiration stream and\nthe photosynthetic processes respectively.\t Thesq materials flow\n( through growth) to the leaves, stem, glumes , fruit and roots.\nVarious losses may occur as a result of insect damage and thenatural\nplant processes, i,e. senescence and abeciss4on in response to\nphysiologipal stress.\t Redistribution (mining) of nitrogen within the ,\nplant is modeled.\t The initiation of organs on the plant occurs as a\nseries of discrete events., with initiation rates depending on\n\nr^\n\ntempera:uv^ and the physiological status of the plant.\nin general, the plantis responses to environmentalfactors are as\nfollows:\t \'Jhotosynthesis depends on light intensity and light intercep-\n\ntion, and, it is reduced by water stress and very low leaf nitrogen\nconcentrations.\t\n\nRespiration depends on temperature and plant biomass.\n\nGrowth is a function of temperature, tissue turgor and metabolite\nsupply.\t Thus, plant water status is a determinant of both supply and\ndemand for i vetAbolites.\t Water stress reduces photosynthesis,\ntranspiration, and nitrogen uptake,\t It also (at a different level of\nstress) reduces growth and the demand for nutrients.\t The\nsupply:dem3nd ratios For carbohydrate and nitrogen are used as indices\nof stress induced organ abscission.\t Here, we assume that the\nmetabolite suppl._y:demand status of the plant determines (or shifts)\nhormone balanges which result in the abscission of organs. \t Thus, a\nsevere moisture stress which interferes with photosynthesis and\n\nL\t\n\n5\n\nnutrient uptake may result in significant fruit abortion, while a mild\nmoisture stress which reduces growth (demand) more than (supply)\n\xe2\x80\xa2 photosynthesis may have no effect or even a positive effect on fruit\nretention.\nWINTER WHEAT gains its broad ecological range, i.e. its\ncapability to simulate crops on virtually any soil type, through the\nF\t\n\nincorporation of RHIZOS.\t RHIZOS (Lambert and Baker, 1982, Whisler\n\n4\t\n\net.al ., 1981) is a comprehensive simulator of the soil processes,\nincluding root growth.\t While the WINTER WHEAT source listing included\nhere (Appendix a) includes the RHIZ06 section, a detailed description\nis not provided, (ref. Lambert and Baker, 1982). \t "RHIZOS" is the name\ngiven to a system of subroutines designed to serve as a general\nrhizosphere model for all crow providing the above ground sections\nwith three parameters; an effective soil grater potential used to\ncalculate plant water potential, an estimate of metabolite sink\nstrength in the roots,` and a mineral nutrient uptake rate.\nThe appendix contains a source listing, a typical input data set,\ndictionary of terms, and a typical output listing. \t The source con-\n\nt\t\n\ntains many comments both to make it readable and to cite everyone who\ncontributed ideas or data either via publications or personal communications.\t There are m\nieny,\t To facilitate program development and up-\n\ndating, labelled rmw\'onri were chosen as a mearts of passing information\nin andout of subroutines.\t Just after- the first block of labelled\nc=TK)ns (ref, Appendix a; A block data section appears in which ttie\nvariables are initialized.\t\n\nThese variables are arranged by rxm ber. of\n2\n\nrf\np\t\n\na\n\nt\nf\n\nt^\n\n!\n\ncharacters and listed alphabetically for accessibility.\nThe Subroutines\nMAIN\nA simplified flowcharting of the model appears in Figure 1. A detailed flowcharting labelled MAIN Program follows. MAIN calls the sub-\n\nroutines and performs a few calculations pertaining mostly to\ninput/output. First, several sta te variables describing the plant are\ninitialized. Then, the initial ;leaf and root weights are read in in teractively from the terminal (device 1). A few c+aTputations pertain\nfng to the initial status of the plant are )Wade, and teen a number of\nother agronomic inputs are read from- the terminal and from the -data\nfile (device 5) Soil parameters are set up and initial soil:\nconditions are defined in the soil. matrix. Then, the climate data are \t\nread in from the data file ( device 5).\n\nF\n\nAt this point the sim+;lation begins, and MAIN calls the process\n\nsubroutines daily. CLYMAT calls the subroutines DATE and TMPSOL.\nSOIL calls most of the RBIZOS subroutines. They produce soil water\npotentials and the amount of nitrate taken up by the plant each day.\nThe daily ncrement of dry atter produced is calculated in PNET\nand distributed the various growingpoints in the plant in the subto\nroutine GROAT . Q?OWTH, in turn, calls RUTGRO a subroutine which calculates root growth. GROWTH also calculates the carbohydrate stress\nand calls _Nrr^Q which cal malate nitrogen stress and allocates to the\t\nes\nvarious plant parts the nitrogen which has been taken up. \t\nAll morphogenetic processes, as well as records of the abortion\nof tillers and fruit, are handled in MDRPH.\n\nt\nk\n\nCLYMAT\n\nEa h day\'s maximum and minimum temperatures in degrees Celsius\nare provided as input to the model. CL,YMAT converts rainfall data from\ninches to millimeters. Empirical relationships based on data\noollected in Mississippi over cotton are used to estimate net\nradiation from solar radiation, and to estimate the average\ntemperatures during daytime and nighttime from the maximum and minimum\ntemperature data\xe2\x80\x9e Note that these relationships (especially the\naverage temperature functions) are location specific. They should be\nvalidated for each site where the model is used.\nCanopy light interception is calculated in CLYMAT. The model\n\ndefines interception as the product of two terms The f trst is a\t\nground cover term, simply the maximum leaf length divided by the row\nwidth. The second is a canopy light attenuation term based on leaf\narea index. The coefficient, 0.4, was taken from Monteith (1965).\nThis canopy light interception model has not been validated.\nFinally, CLYMAT calls DTSOL which calculates soil profile temperatures at 2, 4 8 and 16 inch depths from regression equations of\nMcWhorter and Brooks ( 1965). These equations express soil temperature \t\nas linear functions of the running average of air temperature (over\nthe preceding 7 days). These empirical relationships were developed\nby McWhorter in a fine textured clay soil in Mississippi. They do not\naccount for soil moisture effects an soil temperature.\n\n-\n\nt`\n\n3\t\n\nF.\n\nt\nx\n\nj is\n\n--qc/\n\nORIGINA1. (1981) for detailed descriptions of\nthe subroutines callers from SOIL. \t However, a brief statement of funstion is offered here.\t In general, the p poses of the RHIZOS section\nof, WINTER WIREA,T are as follows:\n(a)\t To provide the plant with mineral nutrients (especially\nnitrogen).\n(b)\t To provide soil water potential information from the root\nzone for the calculation of plant turgor levels and leaf water\npotentials.\t The loaf water potentials, in turn, are used to\nestimate water stress induced Leauct:ions in growth.\n(c)\t To provide \'the above ground model with an estimate of the\nroot sink stre.,gth for carbon and nitrogen compounds.\nRHIZOS, a two dimensional model, considers a cross section of the\nsoil under one row.\t Both dimensions of the section are variable, the\nwidth being row width, two metiers being the depth.\t This section is\none cm thick and it is assumed to be longitudinally representative of\nthe row.\t It is subdivided into a 6x20 matrix. \t It keeps a daily\nd ammonium nitrogen and root.\nmateralf in each^oelloof thermatrixate^ age\t\nof root mass is\nmaintained .and used to estimate root growth and water uptake.\nfertilizer may be added at any depth. \t If fertilizer is to be\nadded on a given day, FRTLIZ is called.\nif rainfall or irrigation occurs, GRAFLO is called which distributes the water vertically in the profile. \t Ammonium ions are assumed\nto be adsorbed on soil colloids and to be stationary.\t Nitrate nitrogen, on the other hand, is assumed to be in solution and to move \t with\nthe soil water.\nAn evapotranspiration routine (ET) adapted from Ritchie (1972) is\nused to provide an empirical estimate of water removed from the\nprofile each day.\t This amount of water, then, is simply imposed on\nthe UPTAKE subroutine.\nDuring stage I drying, water is removed from the sunlit cells of\nthe top layer of the matrix in UPTAKE.\nTranspiration losses occur in those; cells containing roots. \t The\namount taken from any given cell depends on the amount and age distribution_ (permeabil tai) of the roots in the cell.\nRgdistribution of water within the soil profile occurs in CAPFLO.\nAgain, nitrate nitrogen moves with the moving soil water\'.\nThy, mineralization of \'organic nitrogen and the conversion of ammonium n trc ^3 to nitrate occurs in NITRIF.\n\nur\n\n3\n\n-\'\n\nPNEr\n\nAs noted earlier., WINTER WHEAT is a materials balance model,\ni.e., each \'day of the growing season an increment of dry . matter is\nproduced and distributed to the growing points in the plant, the end\npoint yield, then, being the dry weight of the grain.\nIn a review of the subject of canopy photosynthesis\n(Baker, et.al ., 1978a) a number of factors were considered in the\nchoice of approach to the problem of estimating canopy photosynthesis.\n\no\'\t\n\n13\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\ns\n\n`\t\n\n1\n\nORIGINAL PAGE IS\nOF POOR QUALITY\nSOIL Subroutine\n\nNotee\n\nFlowchart\n7\n\nCLIMAT(e) is the amount of fertilizer to be\napplied today:\n\nfLitN n CLINAT ( 8)\n\n,F\n\nTrue\n\nIDAY.GT.l\n\nOn the first day of the simulation, call the\nfertiliser subroutine to add nitrogen found\nin the organic matter, and \' to add residual\nnitrate and smr nium to the profile.\n\nFalse\nCALL FRTLIZ\n_\n\ns\n\n3\nutput the value of the\nF\n\nvariable\n\nVNO3C(i.l)\n\nOKA \xe2\x80\xa2 O\t\n\nAfter the first daK the organic matter and\nresidual nitrate and ammonium variablf4 are\n\n-\n\nW to zero.\n\nf\n\nRNNO3 = 0\nRNNRL n O\nCONTINUE\n\ni\n\nt\n\nFalse\n\nFERN . NOM\n\ni\n\nR\n\nto\n\ngram per ,plant..\n\n.1/ROHSP\n\nDo the nitrification process*&\n\n1\n\ni.\n\nThe static models of Monsi and Saeki (1953), deWit (1965), Duncan,\net.al,.\t (1967), and Tooming, (1967) consider the leaf as the basic\nphotosynthetic element.\t They treat an exceeding ly oamplex subject re quiring a vast amount of input data describing the physical location,\ntbo climate and the angular orientation of each leaf elemen t in the\ncanopy.\t This information must be provided continuously throughout the\nday.\t In order to accurately estimate total canopy performance they\nalso require the age, the developmental h istory and the current\nnw,\xe2\x96\xba tritional status of each leaf element.\t All this can be provided in\na model,. but at considerable expense.\nin addition to the complexity involved, these static leaf element\nprese nt\ncrop modeler with three other difficcllti;es.\t l\xe2\x80\xa2irst.,\n\tteever been validated.\t The best that has been done is\nnone\t\nrls\nto compare theta with weekly dry matter accumulation data - which is\nson*what analogous to using a calendar rather than a stop watch to\nmeasure the pulse rate of a heart patient.\t Secondly, they do not: correctly account for respiration. \t They simply assume that some fixed\nThis becomes a\nfraction of photosynthate is consumed in respiration,\t\nfatal error in the attempt to use these static models in a dynamic\nform since respiration is a function of quantity of biamass.. \t Finally,\nthey assume \'a horizontally uniform distribution of leaves which is not\nappropriate in a row crop.\nWith effort all of these difficulities could have been overcome,\nbut the result wed, at best, have been a rather inconsistent patch\nn\njob.\t We chose instead to take a more empirical approach, tre a ti g the\n\nj\nj\n\nentire, plant canopy as the photosynthetic element.\t There is abundant\n\nprecedent for this in the literature (Baker, et.al., 1978a:, and, it\nleads more -directly and more precisely to the quantity of dry matter\nproduced by the crop.\t It depends, however, on the availability of a\nt of canopy photosynthesis-respiration data in a crop of known biomass.\nA detailed flow chart of Pt= is presented an pages 17 and 18.\nThe model does not contain a mechanism for the calculation of leaf\nwater potential from environmental inputs, ar.;d so it (PSzL) is simply\nset equal to five times the water potential in the rooted portion of\nthe soil profile.\t The next several statements, down to line 10,\ncomputo a water stress reduction factor for photosynthesis. \t The reduction factor (PTSRED) is a linear function of leaf water potential\ntaken directly from Figure 1 of 4awlor (1976).\t We believe that the\ndata base for PTSRED must be confitmed in experiments at various\nstages of development in crops grown under natural light and with var-\n\nj\n\n-M\n\nious patterns of water stress development.\nNext, c=Vy photosynthesis, on a ground area basis, is calculated. The respiration data are\npresented in Figure 2.\t Two techniques were used in these\nmeasurements.\t In the first, (figure 2a) the chamber was quickly\ndarkened after a period of photosynthesis.\t in the second, (Figure 2b)\nthe chamber was kept dark for a period of about 18 hours prior to and\nduring the respiration measurements. \t Rate of i-ncrWe in canopy cc)\n\nw\t\n\nwas measured after 25 to 30 minutes" acconadation to a 0e \'t\n-wrat3re\nlevel.\t Unlike the results with cotton, (Baker et.al., 1970 we found\nno difference in rate of canopy respiration whether preceded by a\nperiod of rapid photosynthesis or not.\t The senesced (SPAR C data\npoints were deleted.\t The light and dark data sets were combined and\nfitted to provide the respiration .Function in the code. This technique\nmay be criticized since it is, in fact, a respiration measurement made\nin +,-,he dark being used to represent respiration in the light, c.f.\nCtvjllet and Ogren (1975). \t Although we believe any quantitative error\n\t small,, this estimate of the respiratory lose in the\nwill be\nbe\nlight will probably be on the high side.\t Convin (1970)\t presents\nevidence that dark respiration may be reduced,in the presence of\nlight. There appeared to be no change in photosynthetic efficiency\nduring the season until the beginning of senescence.\t The data were\ncollected on crops in three SPAR units maintained at three temperature\nregimes (c.f. Table l). \t 5o, the crops matured at different rates.\nThe effect of senescence on canopy photosynth\t is Is shown in Figure\n3.\t There was no significant; senescence effect in chamber R through\ndays 114, 116,. and 117, nor was any senescence in A noticeable through\ndays 126, 127,\t and 128.\t Appropriate dark respiration val\'ues from the\nabove .measurements were added to these (fifteen minute) apparent\nphotosynthesis values, and, the data were pooled and fitted to obtain\na composite canopy light response curve with 258 15-minute data\npoints. An R\t value of 0.89 was obtained.\t This curve was used, with\n15-rainute average solar radiation data throughout the daylight periods\nin 36 representative days over the season to produce the daily total\ndata presented in Figure 4. The data range from completely clear days\nto completely and heavily overcast days.\t The equation for this curve\nis used to rnlculate daily photosynthate production (PSTAND) from\ndaily total solar radiation in WINTER WHEAT. (1974) and Friend et.al . (1962).\t Root\ngrowth is handled in RUTGRO, a RHIZOS subroutine, which is call ed\ntwice from GROWTH.\t In RUTGRO the soil water potential in those parts\nof the soil profile containing roots is used along with climate inforur\nation tii calculate day time and night time (WSTRSD AND WSTRSN) water\nstress parameters referred to below.\nGrowth strategy is as follows:\na)\t the plant is inventoried and a potential growth rate for each\n\n;\n\na\n\nof the organs is calculated as a function of temperature, assuming no\n\nshortage of photosynthate or nitrogen. \t A total carbohydrate demand\n(CD) is calculated as the sum of the potential growth increments of\nall the plant organs. \t Plant attributes used in this calculation include organ weights and ages (since initiation). \t When abetter organ\ndata base is available, potential growth will be calculated for day\nand night time periods separately using tet^perature and water stress\ninputs appropriate to those time periods.\n\nF\n\nb)\t after the calculation of potential carbohydrate requirements,\n\nthe NITRO subroutine is called from GROWTH.\t NITRO will be described\nin detail later. Its function is to estimate the nitrogen required to\nassimilate the amount of carbon just estimated for each of the organs.\nThese nitrogen requirements are sunned for the vegetative parts and\nthe fruiting parts and the sums are used in the denominators of\nnitrogen supply/demand ratios to estimate the maximum fractions of the\ncarbohydrate uptake potentials that can actually be assimilated,\nconsidering the nitrogen limitations. \t This, then, is a reduced or\nrefined estimate of potential organ growth increments.\nc)\t a carbohydrate supply/demand ratio is calculated as follows:\n\n".\nh\nG\n,i\n\nx\n\nCPOOL = PN + RESC\nCSTRES = CPOOL/CD\n\nwhere CPOOL is the total available pool of carbohydrate from today\'s\nincrement of photosynthate production, plus reserve carbon carried in\nfrom earlier dogs, and CSTRES is the carbohydrate supply:demand ratio.\nd)\t actual growth of each organ on the plant, then, is calculated;\nas the product r?f potential growth multiplied by CSTRES. \t This partitions photosynthate to each organ on the plant in proportion to its\ncontribution to total demand, except that grain will receive their\nfull requirement first if sufficient carbohydrate is available for\ngrain growth.\t Anything beyond that is partitioned to the vegetative\nparts, including roots.\nThe water stress terms,\nGROWTH is flowcharted on pages 25 to 30\t\n\na\n\nt\n\nq\n\n?\n\na\n\n24\nt\n\n-\n\nG\n\ntr\t\n\n,\n\n4\ni\na <Z\nC\n\nORIGINAL, PAGC IS\n^^\n\nOF POOR QUALITY\nGROWTH Subroutine\n, 0025\n\nWTP\t\n\n\xe2\x80\x9eWTF\n\nis the factor to convert leaf\narea in Sw,\xe2\x96\xba +2 to weight in grams.\n\nnI\'WST a (WSTRSD a DAYTYM +\nVSTRSN k NYTTYM)/ 24.\nDO 100 1\t\n\nDetermine the water stress factor for\nreduction tof potential growth.\n\n1, NST EMS\n\nUo for all the stems on th^i plant.\n\nPDSTEM (I) - 0.1\n\nInitialize the potential change in\nstem weight for each stem. The\nsecond statement forms a water stress factor from water stress data\n(WSTI\'CZSO and W5TRSN) data brought in Lrpm K\nvia MAIN. These data\nrepresent the .fraction of the day and night time periods during which\nthe leaf water potential is estimated to be above -7 bars. The remaining statements on page 25 calculate or define the potential dry matter\n=30\n\nt\t\n\n{\n\nt\t\n{\t\n\naccumulation increments in the stems, glumes and grain. The values for\nstems and glumes have been chosen arbitrarily. The values for the\ngrain are taken from Sof,ield et.al . (1974). These are first defined \t\nfor the heading stage. Then, they are soccessively defined for the\njointing, booting and anthesis (plus 4 days) stages.\nThe :statements on page 26 and down to statement 120 on page 27 define the potential growth increment of each leaf on each stem as _functions of temperature and water stress. The data baser both for leaf\t\ngrowth rake and the length of the leaf\' growth period is from Friend,\net.a .. (1962). They did not record leaf growth per se The temperature responses represent total above ground vegetative growth rates.\nTheir experiments were done with Marquis wheat (Triticum\naestivum).undev artificial light (up to a maximum of 2500 f.c.), and\ntheir data emend only to 30 C.. We believe that values derived from\nthis da t a set may be low representations of "potential", i.e. not\nlimited by carbohydrate supply, growth. Certainly these data need to\nbe confirmed in further experiments. However, we have u_aed the Friend\t\net.al . data only to construct the shape of a temperature response.\nActual amounts of :Leaf growth appear to be reasonable. First, leaf\narea growth is calculated.rrLning length of the leaf growth period\nthe\n\t (since leaf initiation) average\nis calculated as a function\ntemperature. Finally, a potential leaf weight increment is calculated\nfrom the potential area growth increment, the specific leaf weight\nfactor and the water stress reduction factor. These potential leaf\ngrowth increments are then accumulated.\nNext potential growth increments for stems, glumes and grain are\nadjusted for water stress and accumulated. Then, (mi.ddle of page 27)\n\na\n\nj\n\nt\n\nt\nthe RHIZZOS cells is computed as eafunction ofsoilotanperaturee and aceR\ncumulated.\nThis potential total root growth increment is added to the total\nof growth increments for stems, leaves, and glumes to produce a total\ncarbohydrate demand (CD) for vegetative growth. Then, the carbohydrate pool is calculated as the sum of today\'s photosynthate production plus reserves carried over from yesterday.\nNext, the supply,-,demand ratio for grain growth is calculated.\nThe following logic allows carbohydrate shortage to terminate vegetative growth entirely in favor of grain growth. -First, the reserve.\npool is decremented by the amount needed for grain growth. If this\ncompletely depletes the reserves, then, reserves are set to zero, the\nsupply-.demand ratio for vegetative growth is zeroed, and, the\nsupgly:demand ratio for fruit growth is defined less than one. If,\nhowever, reserves are not depleted by grain growth, they are\n\n31\n\xe2\x80\xa21\n\n4\ni\n\ndecree tinted by the amount needed for vegetative growth. if they are\nstill not depleted, full vegetative growth occurs. if they are\ndepleted, a supply:demand ratio for vegetative growth less than one is\ncalculated.\nNext, NITRO is called and in an analogous way, nitrogen\nsupply:demand ratios for grain and vegetative growth are calculated.\nAfter return from NITRO, (muddle of page 28 through page 29)\nactual dry matter growth of each organ on the plant in calculated. In\neach case, three .steps are taken. First, the new organ size is\ndefined as the old value plus today\'s increment,: which is %pal to the\npotential growth increment multiplied by the supply:demand ,ratios for\n\ncarbohydrate and: nitrogen. Nlext, if nitrogen was limiting, same carbohydrate is left over, (XIRAi ).. This is accumulated and added to reserve. Finally, the total weights of the various categories of organs\nare accumulated.\nAfter RUTGRO is called for the actual incrementing of root dry\nnatter, total plant weight and vegetative weight, are calculated. Maxi\nmum leaf length is calculated for use in the estimation of canopy\nground, cover (Wr) p and LAI is calculated\n\nL\t\n\'t\'\t\n\n`\t\n\nRUTGRO\nThis subroutine calc-1ates potential and actual dry matter in the\nvarious parts of the root system.\t It also calculates water stress parameters which are used. in GROWTH to adl\t t potential growth of above\nground plant parts.\nA more detailed description of this subroutine is \t p\tresented b x\np\t tion\nLambert and Balser (1982) in their discussion of RHIZOS. \t The parts\ndirectly affecting above gr,aund processes will be outlined here for\nreadability of the present SUscussion, _of WINTER WHEAT as a whole.\nFlow charts are presented ors, pages 33-44.\nThe water stress param\'.4ters (WSTLRSD and MTRSN) are calculated\nfirst and will therefore be presented first in this discussion.\nBoyer (1970) presents data showing an abrupt cessation in leaf\ngrowth in soybean, sunflower and corn as leaf water potential falls\nfrom about\'-3 bars (full turgor).\t The exact cutoff varies with\nspecies and we presume it varies with\t conditioning.\t The plants approach zero enlargement asymptotically, reaching zero at or before\n-12 bars leaf water potential. \t Baker et. al. (1982) have chosen thresholds ranging from -3 to -12 bars and found that a -7 bar threshold\nworks best for estimating growth in cotton.\t This analysis has yet to\nbe repeated for winter wheat.\n!Model strategy is to assume that above -7 bars leaf water potential there is no :restriction to growth of above ground plant parts and\nbelow that threshold no growth occurs, since the asymptote is approached sharply in Boyer\'s data.\t A regression model expressing\ncotton leaf water potential, (PSIL) as a function of \'soil water\t potential (PSIS), net radiation (RN) and temperature (TA), where water\npotentials ark in bars, temperature is.in Celsius, and net radiation\nis in watts/m\t was used to calculate PSIS, values at ten minute\nintervals for all combinations of the weather and soil water potential\nconditions in Table 2. \t Daily time courses of a typical data set are\ngiven in Figure 5 along with the net radiation and air temperature\ngu\t\ng\t\nvalues Day and night time water stress terms WSTRSD and WSTRSN\nrespectively, are calculated using the average nail suction in the\n.rooted portion of the profile. While this data base and procedure are\nused in the present WINTER WHEAT model, we emphasize that a data base\nfrom winter wheat, and, possibly a more mechanistic model would be\nmore appropriate.\nIn the calculation of potential root growth in each of the cells\nin the RHIZOS matrix, we assume exponential growth based on the mass\nof roots present in an age category capable of growth. Good data on\nthe effects of kemperature and carbohydrate supply on root growth\nrates in winter wheat were not available, and so cotton data (GOSSYM,\ns,\nBaker et. al ., 1982) have been used. Thu g the RC10M parameter is\nobtained by the same function as for yoking cotton bolls. Subsequent\nwoA by Whisler et.al .,(1977) working with GOSSYM, in which they\nsimulated the root growth measured in SPAR units by Phene et.al .\n(1978) showed that potential root growth is in fact an order of\nmagnitude greater than potential boll growth on a weight of growing\ntissue basis. Other subsequent analysas by Fye et.al. (1982) have\nshown the ROOTXP term must be multiplied by factors of five or six to\n\nsimulate field_ crops. Clearly this is an unacceptably crude\' guess as\n\nto the potential dry matter accretion rates in winter wheat .roots.\nControlled environment research on winter wheat roots is indicated.\nAfter calculation of the ROOM term, the model calculates a pot ential (PDMT) root growth value for each cell from the root weight\n\ncapable of growth (RZWIC\'G) thus,\nPDWRT - R74TCG\'kROOTXP.\n\nj\n\nd\n\nThen, these are summed over the whole root system to form a total.\n(SPDWR\ninally the model returns to RUTGRO from GROWTH where an inc rement of carbohydrate actually to be allotted to the root system is determined.\t This dry matter is partitioned to each part of the root system in proportion to its contribution to total demand,\n\n,\n\nRGCP = RGH2O/SPDWRT.\nFinally the root growth correctionfactor (RGCF) is multiplied by the\npotential root growth terms (PDhRT) to give an increment of dry matter\n\naccumulation (DhRT) in each cell.\nNITRO\n\n,E\n\nThis\nvolved in the partitioning of mtetabol iittesiin the plan The supply,\nl\non a particular day, consists of the increment of nitrogen brought in\nthrough the root system (UPTAKE) plus mobilizable reserves.\t Three\ntypes of constants pertaining to NITRO are read in from the keyboard\nwhen operating from a computer terminal.\t These are a nitrogen reserve\n\nE\t\n\ng\n\n46\n;r\n\n^\n\nr\n\na\n\n,r\n\nF\n\nmobilization factor (F2), "K" factors representing the minimum percentage of tissue dry matter occurring as nitrogen after all reserves have\nbeen withdrawn, and "J" factors representing the minimum nitrogen concentration of new dry matter added to organs.\t F2 is arbitrarily set\nat 0.5.\t Usually "K" factors for leaves, stems, roots and glumes are\nall set at 0.01, and all "J" factors for leaves,_ stew, roots, glumes\nand grain are set at 0.03.\t Obviously, these values are arbitrarily\n\nchosen and need to be verified; experimentally.\n\n,\n\nNITRO is flow charted or. pages 49-51.\t Organ weights and nitrogen\ncontents are brought in.\t Also brought in (from qtOt+M) are potential\ngrowth increments.\t The nitrogen supply:demand;ratios .are initialized,\nand, reserves are calculated as the difference between the tissue nitrogen content and the content it could go dorm to if all reserves were\nwithdrawn.\t Reserves in the various classes of organs are added to get\na total reserve (RESN). If the nitrogen required for growth of all organs is greater than the pool, stress\nfactors are calculated as follows:\t if the pool is large enough for:\nfull-rrrain growth., the vegetative growth stress factor is defined as\nthe difference between the total pool.and the grain growth\nrequirement ? all divided by the vegetative growth requirement, and the\nstress term for fruit growth (NF) remains one. \t If, haw!ever, the grain\ngr\t\nequi\t\n9r\t\ngrowth requirement is greater\n\t the pool, NF is defined as the peal\ndivided by the grain requirement, and W is set to zero.\nNext, the nitrogen contents of each of the classes of organs ;i\nupdated and a total plant nitrogen content is calculated.\t If more nitrogen was taken up than was used in structural growth, the extra N is\nstored in the various vegetative structures in proportion to their\nfraction of the total vegetative dry weight.\t If there was a deficit\nof nitrogen (required over what was taken up), the deficit amount is\nwithdrawn from reserves (negative addition of XTRAN).\nFinally, the leaf nitrogen concentration is calculated for use in\nMORPH.\n\nI\n\nI\n\nE\n\ni\n\n_]\n\ni\n\nMORPH\n\nu\t s\n\nThis subroutine simulates plant mmorphogenesi....\t It handles system\ntiming and the abortion of tillers and fruit in response to physiological stresses.\t It records, daily, the census of organs on the plant\nand their maturity status.\t MORPH is flowcharted on pages 52-64. Maturityr , (TTGF),\'and the\ntermination of the simulation is determined as a linear function of\nrunning average temperature from anthesis. \t The data base for time of\ngrain maturity is from Sofield et.al . (1977).\nReferring to the flow charts beginning on page 53,\t if tillering\nhas not yet begun, the computer is directed to statement 100 (page 54)\nwhere secondary roots and leaves are initiated. \t Heat units are accumilated from one secondary root initiation; event to the next. The time\nbetween secondary root events is a function of the running average temperature.\t The function is arbitrarily chosen, and needs to be confirmed by further, controlled environment experiments. \t If sufficient\ntime has elapsed, and, soil water potential is greater than -1 bar in\nthe rooted portion of the soil., a secondary root is added.\nNext, running average temperatures for each stem and for each\nleaf are updated.\t Time intervals betw een new leaf initiation events\nare a function of n\xe2\x80\x9etnning average temperature, with a minimum time of\n4 days.\t The. data base for this temperature-time interval relationship is froth f igures l and 2 of Friend et.al . (1962, p1299).\nAfter this leaf initiation, if tillering has not begun, the computer\nwill default all further logic to the end of the subroutine.\nReferring again to page 52, if 315 heat units (ACC;DEW have accumulated, differentiation begins. \t if 750 heat unitshave accumulated,\ntillering ends, and jointing begins, and the computer checks to see if\ntime for jointing (1090 heat units) has passed. \t If so, it chPbks to\nsee if the time for heading has arrived.\t The times of jointing,\nbooting, heading, anthesis, and maturity of each of the stems are\nrecorded separately. The time spread auong the stems in booting,\njointing and heading is maintained the same as that established in\njointing.\nSecondary root development occurs through the tillering, jointing\nand booting periods.\t Each primary tiller is capable of producing more\ntillers (up to three each), if the primary tiller has at least three\nleaves\'. The time required to produce these secondary tillers is a function of the running average\'temperature since the last (secondary tiller) was initiated.\t This function (bottom of page 56) has been chosen\narbitrarily.\t We note that in view of the fact that a tiller may be\naborted very quickly after it is initiated, it is very difficult\nto nxiasure initiation rates except ander conditions not favoring\nabortion.\t A great need exists here for further controlled environment\nresearch characterizing the rates of tillering and tiller abortion in\ndependently.\t When a new tiller is initiated, the leaf number associated with it is initialized to 1. Leaf number is limited to six per\nstem. _If, the plant has less than four primary tillers (top of page\n58), none will be aborted.\t However, if more exist, a newly initiated\ntiller will be aborted if either soil water_ potential in the root zone\nis less than -1.0 bar, :leaf nitrogen concentration is less than three\npercent, or if any carbohydrate stress exists. \t The tiller will also\nbe aborted if, after spring green up, leaf water, potential is below\n-20.0 bar or there are less than four secondary roots per tiller.\nDifferentiation of all beads 6 ,.-curs at the same time (.i.e. on the\nday of accumu lat ion of 315 heat un i\'l :s) , regardless of the age of the\n\na\n\na\n\nr\n\ni\n65\n\nf`\n\n$\t\n-\n\n}\n\n"a\n\ntiller. The number of florets per spikelet is set at u^\t Variation\nirk kernel immber occurs only via variation in the number of spikelets\nper head, except that for the first three days after anthesis, florets\nmay be lost from a particular head through dessication.\t Spikelet number may be reduced from a maximum of 22 per head either by carbohydrate or nitrogen shortage. \t Reductions up to 50 percent will occur in\nproportion to reserve- carbohydrate levels below 6 percent of leaf dry\nweight.\t Additional reductions up to 50 percent will occur in proportion to leaf nitrogen concentrations below 4 percent. \t This approach\nto the calculation of kernel number may be criticized on several\ngrounds.\t First, as Klepper (1980, pers. comm.) has noted, differentiation of all heads does not occur at the same time. \t Each head is differentiated when that tiller reaches the appropriate physiological age.\nSecond, floret number is not constant among all spikelets.\t After the\nrachis is laid out, spikelet initiation begins about 35 percent of the\nway up the rachis and proceeds both up and down over a period of a\nmonth or so.\t During that time florets are initiated from the primary\nfloret in each spikelet outward. \t During this time florets may be aborted due to physiological stresses, the younger being aborted first.\nThus, the spikelets at the top and bottom of the head, typically, contain fewer florets.\t Finally, the data base for the abortion of\nflorets in response to physiological stress is completely inadequate\nat present (although it - can be developed via a routine and orderly experimental effort) indicating the need for a completely different differentiation model, and for a set of experiments in which heads are\nmapped, in time, over a range of temperatures, photosynthate and nitrogen supply levels,\nAt the top of page 61, all stems to be jointed must start\njointing within 15 days of the first.\t An arbitrarily chosen oaq:)asite\nvariable which is a function of water stress, carbohydrate stress and\nnitrogen stress is used to determine whether one, two or three stems\nwill begin jointing on the particular day. \t This ;logic is crude, but\nthe model is not particu\'2,arly sensitive to it, and it provides a means\nof spreading, in time, the jointing process in response to factors of\nknown importance.\t There will be a maximum of seven joints in the\nelongated stem.\nAfter 1300 heat units are accumulated, the first stem begins anthesis.\t The remaining heads begin anthesis the same number of days\nlater as occurred in jointing.\t For three days after the beginning of\nanthesis in a head, florets may be dessicated if the average (over the\nday) leaf water potential falls below -15 bar.\t Dessication is limited\nto 25 percent of the florets per head per day. \t Finally, the number of\nflorets per head reaching maturity is limited to 60; and,, it cannot\nfall below 10.\t ,Again, experimental verification of the hater stress\nlevels andother factors contributing to dessication at mthesis is\nneeded.\n\n`\'\n\nf\n\'\n\nC\n\nI\n9\n\ng\n\nConclusions and Future Research Needs\nf this\nis to docu ent the basic ideas and conThe\nT\t\nof this\t m\nstruc\t\nfor\t r\t ose\nphysic\ncal/physiological process level winter\nwheat simulation model, and to assess the adequacy of the information\n\nxi>\nt\n\nf\n\n66\n\nbase (published literature, unpublished results, theses, etc.) for\nsuch a model.\t In constructing this model, we have found that while\nall of the data necessary may be obtained by certain well established\nexperimental methods, by and large they do not now exist. \t Here, we\noutline the further research needed, process, by process, as we now\nsee it.\n\nData needs, here, can generally be classified either as thresholds (e,g. minimum levels of tissue nitrogen which can be drawn on\nreserve basis to fulfill needs in other parts of the plant), or\nprocess rate coefficients. Nearly all of these data can be obtained in\ncontrolled environment experiments. \t The SPAR unit (Phene et.al.,\n1918, MdKinion, 1980) has been designed expressly for this purpose.\nMore ;SPAR units are needed at Mississippi State and at several other\nlocations .involved in the development of this model.\nThe model presented here does not contain a mechanism for the cal-\n\ntulation of leaf water potential.\t Such a mechanism is \'being incorpor-\n\nated by Parton and others now at Fort Collins.\t Leaf water potential\nis used in estimating most of the plant process rates, including photosynthesis.\t The data base for the water stress reduction in photosynthesis must be confirmed in experiments at all stages of development\nin crops grown under Natural light.\t A ^aariety of patterns of development of water stress should be studied. \t The effecC of leaf nitrogen\nti\n.\nph\t p\t\npy r^hotos^.^nthetic efficienc \t\nandos horous levels on canop y\t^ y muss, be\nmeasured.\t The effect of starch \'buildup on canopy photosynthesis must\n\t light capture\nbe measured.\t The effect of stand geometry on can\nmust be characterized .\t. The latter can :best be done in field\n\n,.\n\nplantings.\nThis relationships between temperature, and dry matter accretion\nrates in each class of organ must be worked. out.\t The tissue water,\npotential level below which growth ceases must be defined for each\nkind of organ.\t These experiments must include root observations.\nIn addition to the root growth measurements at various temperatures,\n\n1\na\n\nthe effect of soij. oxygen concentration and physical i Vedance must be\ncharacterized.\nThree sets of parameters in regard to nitrogen and phosphorous\nare needed,\t the minimum concentration needed for new growth in each\n\ntype of organ, the maximuirs concentration each ,lass of organ can tolerate-, and the minimum concentration to which the plant can reduce each\nclass of organ for use as reserves.\nNeeded: morphogenetic studies include the effect of temperature on\nthe ,rates of secondary root and tiller formation. \t In the tillering\nstudy the e+\' ec\t of physiological stress on tiller abortion should be\nmeasured, and the processes of tiller abortion and tiller initiation\n\nyi\n\nshould be eY\xe2\x96\xbaaraoterizc4d independently. This will require a\nconsiderable amount of destructive sampling in controlled environment\nexperiments as well as a lot of microscope work.\n\nThe present model determines head differentiation at one time\n\n(the day of accumulation of 315 heat units).\t A\nmodel has been written for use in future drafts\n\nhead differentiation\nwhich builds the\nrachis and then elaborates spikelets and florets at rates depending on\nenvironmental conditions, and, aborts florets in response to metabolic\nstresses.\t This model will have to be verified in SPAR experiments\n\n67\n\n,L\n\n\t\n\nG\n\nF\t\nk\t\n\n`\t\n\n{\n\nwhere temperature and the rate of photosynthesis can be controlled independently.\nThe present model does not consider phosphorous nutrition. In\nthe case of nitrogen uptake, only the passive movement of nitrate into\nthe plant via the transpiration stream is simulated. Transpiration\nrates are too low in the seedling stage for this process to provide\nreasonable leaf nitrogen concentrations. Similar results have been reported (Baker, et.al ., 1979) for the cotton model OOSSYM which incorporates the same RHIZOS model. Active uptake of ammonium, nitrate and\nphosphorous is now being incorporated in the UPTAKE subroutine of\nRHI?AS by Cole and Parton. A phosphorous balance model for the plant\nwill be included in the next draft of WIMER WHEAT. These additions\nare required for the new head differentiation model.\nIn a winter winter wheat model fall conditions, hardiness levels,\nsnow cover, root temperature, etc. all need to be considered in\nsimulating winter tiller survival.\nNone of the experiments outlined here are particularly difficult,\nnor do they recp re the development of any new technology. They do\nhowever, require a considerable amount of time and equipment.\n\na\n\noutput\noutput from a typical "run" is included in _Appendix d. It was\nrun with soil physical parameters and weather data for the 1978-79\ngrowing season at Akron, Colorado. Because the form of the model\ndescribed here does not contain a mechanism for the "active" uptake of\nnitrogen, the nitrogen fertilizer input used in the simulation was\ndouble that of the field planting. Reference to the dictionary of\nterms makes the output self explanatory. The first block of output;\ncontains parameters entered by the operator from the terminal. The\nnext block: of output d=1ta lists the input soil parameters. The next\ntwo output block describe the simulated plant and soil system on a\ntime interval selected by the operator and input from the terminal.\nThe first of these blocks describes the plant on the output day. The\nsecond is a graphical depiction of the two dimension distributions Qf\nnitrate nitrogen, root dry matter, and soil water potential. Also\navailable are maps of the ammonium nitrogen and soil water content.\t\nThis output is included simply to suggest the kinds of information the\nmodel provides the user. It does not represent a validation effort,\nand the yield figure is not accurate.\n\n?\n\n9\ni\n\nd\n\ni\n\nx\'\n\nORIGINAL PAGE IS\nOF POOR QUALITY\n\nt\n\nC\nC\nC\nC\nC\n\nBLOCK
